</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:French_language" title="Template:French language"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:French_language" title="Template talk:French language"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:French_language&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>French</b> (<span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">français</i></span> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="fr-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[fʁɑ̃sɛ]</a></span> or <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">langue française</i></span> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="fr-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[lɑ̃ɡ fʁɑ̃sɛːz]</a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European family</a>. It descended from the <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, as did all Romance languages. French evolved from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallo-Romance" title="Gallo-Romance">Gallo-Romance</a>, the Latin spoken in <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a>, and more specifically in Northern Gaul. Its closest relatives are the other <a href="/wiki/Langues_d%27o%C3%AFl" title="Langues d'oïl">langues d'oïl</a>—languages historically spoken in <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Northern_France" title="Northern France">northern France</a> and in southern <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, which French (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francien" title="Francien">Francien</a>) largely supplanted. French was also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substratum" title="Substratum">influenced</a> by native <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a> of Northern Roman Gaul like <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Belgica" title="Gallia Belgica">Gallia Belgica</a> and by the (<a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a>) <a href="/wiki/Frankish_language" title="Frankish language">Frankish language</a> of the post-Roman <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Frankish</a> invaders. Today, owing to France's <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">past overseas expansion</a>, there are numerous <a href="/wiki/French-based_creole_languages" title="French-based creole languages">French-based creole languages</a>, most notably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haitian_Creole_language" title="Haitian Creole language">Haitian Creole</a>. A French-speaking person or nation may be referred to as <b>Francophone</b> in both English and French.
</p><p>French is an <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_where_French_is_an_official_language" title="List of countries where French is an official language">29 countries</a> across multiple continents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> most of which are members of the <i><a href="/wiki/Organisation_internationale_de_la_Francophonie" title="Organisation internationale de la Francophonie">Organisation internationale de la Francophonie</a></i> (OIF), the community of 84 countries which share the official use or teaching of French. French is also one of six official languages used in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> It is spoken as a first language (in descending order of the number of speakers) in France; Canada (especially in the <a href="/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada" title="Provinces and territories of Canada">provinces</a> of <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, Ontario, and <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a>, as well as other <a href="/wiki/French_language_in_Canada#Francophones_across_Canada" title="French language in Canada">Francophone regions</a>); Belgium (<a href="/wiki/Wallonia" title="Wallonia">Wallonia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>-Capital Region); western Switzerland (specifically the cantons forming the <a href="/wiki/Romandy" title="Romandy">Romandy</a> region); parts of <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>; parts of the United States (the states of <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>); <a href="/wiki/Monaco" title="Monaco">Monaco</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Aosta_Valley" title="Aosta Valley">Aosta Valley</a> region of Italy; and various communities elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, approximately 40% of the francophone population (including <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">L2</a> and partial speakers) lived in Europe, 36% in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, 15% in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, 8% in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, and 1% in Asia and <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIF_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-OIF-6">[6]</a></sup> French is the second most widely spoken mother tongue in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nativeLanguages_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-nativeLanguages-7">[7]</a></sup> Of Europeans who speak other languages natively, approximately one-fifth are able to speak French as a second language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> French is the second most taught foreign language in the EU. All institutions of the EU use French as a working language along with English and German; in certain institutions, French is the sole working language (e.g. at the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Justice_of_the_European_Union" title="Court of Justice of the European Union">Court of Justice of the European Union</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> French is also the 18th <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">most natively spoken language</a> in the world, fifth <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_total_number_of_speakers" title="List of languages by total number of speakers">most spoken language by total number of speakers</a> and the second or third most studied language worldwide (with about 120 million learners as of 2017).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> As a result of French and Belgian <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> from the 16th century onward, French was introduced to new territories in the Americas, Africa and Asia. Most second-language speakers reside in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francophone_Africa" title="Francophone Africa">Francophone Africa</a>, in particular <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005_report_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-2005_report-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>French is estimated to have about 76 million native speakers; about 235 million daily, fluent speakers;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethnologuefrench_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-ethnologuefrench-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qu'est-ce_que_la_Francophonie_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Qu'est-ce_que_la_Francophonie-14">[14]</a></sup> and another 77–110 million secondary speakers who speak it as a <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a> to varying degrees of proficiency, mainly in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> According to the OIF, approximately 321 million people worldwide are "able to speak the language",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Francophonie_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Francophonie-16">[16]</a></sup> without specifying the criteria for this estimation or whom it encompasses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ontheuplocalfr2014_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-ontheuplocalfr2014-2">[2]</a></sup> According to a demographic projection led by the <a href="/wiki/Universit%C3%A9_Laval" title="Université Laval">Université Laval</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Agence_universitaire_de_la_Francophonie" title="Agence universitaire de la Francophonie">Réseau Démographie de l'Agence universitaire de la Francophonie</a>, the total number of French speakers will reach approximately 500 million in 2025 and 650 million by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> OIF estimates 700 million by 2050, 80% of whom will be in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OIF_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-OIF-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>French has a long history as an international language of literature and scientific standards and is a primary or second language of many international organisations including the United Nations, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty_Organization" title="North Atlantic Treaty Organization">North Atlantic Treaty Organization</a>, the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">International Committee of the Red Cross</a>. In 2011, <i><a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_Businessweek" title="Bloomberg Businessweek">Bloomberg Businessweek</a></i> ranked French the third most useful language for business, after English and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Mandarin_Chinese" title="Standard Mandarin Chinese">Standard Mandarin Chinese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lauerman2011_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lauerman2011-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Grammar"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Grammar</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Nouns"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Nouns</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Verbs"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Verbs</span></a>
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_French" title="History of French">History of French</a></div>
<p>French is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language">Romance language</a> (meaning that it is descended primarily from <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a>) that evolved out of the Gallo-Romance dialects spoken in northern France. The language's early forms include <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">Middle French</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vulgar_Latin_in_Gallia">Vulgar Latin in Gallia</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallo-Romance" title="Gallo-Romance">Gallo-Romance</a></div>
<p>Due to Roman rule, Latin was gradually adopted by the inhabitants of Gaul, and as the language was learned by the common people it developed a distinct local character, with grammatical differences from Latin as spoken elsewhere, some of which being attested on graffiti.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adams_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adams-19">[19]</a></sup> This local variety evolved into the Gallo-Romance tongues, which include French and its closest relatives, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arpitan_language" title="Arpitan language">Arpitan</a>.
</p><p>The evolution of Latin in Gaul was shaped by its coexistence for over half a millennium beside the native <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaulish_language" title="Gaulish language">Gaulish language</a>, which did not go extinct until the late sixth century, long after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall of the Western Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helix_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Helix-20">[20]</a></sup> The population remained 90% indigenous in origin;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> the Romanizing class were the local native elite (not Roman settlers), whose children learned Latin in Roman schools. At the time of the collapse of the Empire, this local elite had been slowly abandoning Gaulish entirely, but the rural and lower class populations remained Gaulish speakers who could sometimes also speak Latin or Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mufwene_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mufwene-23">[23]</a></sup> The final language shift from Gaulish to Vulgar Latin among rural and lower class populations occurred later, when both they and the incoming Frankish ruler/military class adopted the Gallo-Roman Vulgar Latin speech of the urban intellectual elite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mufwene_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mufwene-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Gaulish language likely survived into the sixth century in France despite considerable <a href="/wiki/Romanization" title="Romanization">Romanization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helix_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Helix-20">[20]</a></sup> Coexisting with Latin, Gaulish helped shape the <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a> dialects that developed into French<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mufwene_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mufwene-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helix_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Helix-20">[20]</a></sup> contributing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loanwords" title="Loanwords">loanwords</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calque" title="Calque">calques</a> (including <i>oui</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> the word for "yes"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Savignac_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Savignac-25">[25]</a></sup> sound changes shaped by Gaulish influence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PellegriniCeltic_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-PellegriniCeltic-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and influences in conjugation and word order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Savignac_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Savignac-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matas_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matas-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adams_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adams-19">[19]</a></sup> Recent computational studies suggest that early gender shifts may have been motivated by the gender of the corresponding word in Gaulish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The estimated number of French words that can be attributed to Gaulish is placed at 154 by the <i><a href="/wiki/Petit_Robert" title="Petit Robert">Petit Robert</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchmittLex_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchmittLex-31">[31]</a></sup> which is often viewed as representing standardized French, while if non-standard dialects are included, the number increases to 240.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Known Gaulish loans are skewed toward certain semantic fields, such as plant life (<i>chêne</i>, <i>bille</i>, etc.), animals (<i>mouton</i>, <i>cheval</i>, etc.), nature (<i>boue</i>, etc.), domestic activities (ex. <i>berceau</i>), farming and rural units of measure (<i>arpent</i>, <i>lieue</i>, <i>borne</i>, <i>boisseau</i>), weapons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HolmesSchutz30_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-HolmesSchutz30-33">[33]</a></sup> and products traded regionally rather than further afield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roegiest82_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roegiest82-34">[34]</a></sup> This semantic distribution has been attributed to peasants being the last to hold onto Gaulish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roegiest82_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roegiest82-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HolmesSchutz30_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-HolmesSchutz30-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_French">Old French</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a></div><p>The beginning of French in Gaul was greatly influenced by Germanic invasions into the country. These invasions had the greatest impact on the northern part of the country and on the language there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-35">[35]</a></sup> A language divide began to grow across the country. The population in the north spoke <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Langue_d%27o%C3%AFl" title="Langue d'oïl">langue d'oïl</a></i></span> while the population in the south spoke <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Langue_d%27oc" title="Langue d'oc">langue d'oc</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-35">[35]</a></sup> Langue d'oïl grew into what is known as Old French. The period of Old French spanned between the 8th and 14th centuries. Old French shared many characteristics with Latin. For example, Old French made use of different possible word orders just as Latin did because <a href="/wiki/Old_French#Nouns" title="Old French">it had a case system that retained the difference between nominative subjects and oblique non-subjects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-36">[36]</a></sup> The period is marked by a heavy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superstrate" title="Superstrate">superstrate</a> influence from the Germanic <a href="/wiki/Frankish_language" title="Frankish language">Frankish language</a>, which non-exhaustively included the use in upper-class speech and higher registers of <a href="/wiki/V2_word_order" title="V2 word order">V2 word order</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> a large percentage of the vocabulary (now at around 15% of modern French vocabulary<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>) including the impersonal singular pronoun <i>on</i> (a calque of Germanic <i>man</i>), and the name of the language itself.
</p><p>Up until its later stages, <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a>, alongside Old Occitan, maintained a relic of the old nominal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Case_system" title="Case system">case system</a> of Latin longer than most other Romance languages (with the notable exception of Romanian which still currently maintains a case distinction), differentiating between an oblique case and a nominative case. The phonology was characterized by a heavy syllabic stress, which led to the emergence of various complicated <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongs</a> such as <i>-eau</i> which would later be leveled to monophthongs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The earliest evidence of what became Old French can be seen in the <a href="/wiki/Oaths_of_Strasbourg" title="Oaths of Strasbourg">Oaths of Strasbourg</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sequence_of_Saint_Eulalia" title="Sequence of Saint Eulalia">Sequence of Saint Eulalia</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Medieval_French_literature" title="Medieval French literature">Old French literature</a> began to be produced in the eleventh century, with major early works often focusing on the lives of saints (such as the <i>Vie de Saint Alexis</i>), or wars and royal courts, notably including the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chanson_de_Roland" title="Chanson de Roland">Chanson de Roland</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Matter_of_Britain" title="Matter of Britain">epic cycles focused on King Arthur and his court</a>, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geste_de_Garin_de_Monglane" title="Geste de Garin de Monglane">a cycle focused</a> on <a href="/wiki/William_of_Gellone" title="William of Gellone">William of Orange</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_French">Middle French</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Middle_French" title="Middle French">Middle French</a></div>
<p>Within Old French many dialects emerged but the <a href="/wiki/Francien_language" title="Francien language">Francien</a> dialect is one that not only continued but also thrived during the Middle French period (14th–17th centuries).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-:02-35">[35]</a></sup> Modern French grew out of this Francien dialect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-:02-35">[35]</a></sup> Grammatically, during the period of Middle French, noun declensions were lost and there began to be standardized rules. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Estienne" title="Robert Estienne">Robert Estienne</a> published the first Latin-French dictionary, which included information about phonetics, etymology, and grammar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Politically, the <a href="/wiki/Ordinance_of_Villers-Cotter%C3%AAts" title="Ordinance of Villers-Cotterêts">Ordinance of Villers-Cotterêts</a> (1539) named French the language of law.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_French">Modern French</span></h3>
<p>During the 17th century, French replaced <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> as the most important language of diplomacy and international relations (<a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a>). It retained this role until approximately the middle of the 20th century, when it was <a href="/wiki/International_English" title="International English">replaced by English</a> as the United States became the dominant global power following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-andaman.org_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-andaman.org-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Stanley Meisler of the <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i> said that the fact that the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a> was written in English as well as French was the "first diplomatic blow" against the language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Si%C3%A8cle" title="Grand Siècle">Grand Siècle</a> (17th century), France, under the rule of powerful leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_Richelieu" title="Cardinal Richelieu">Cardinal Richelieu</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV</a>, enjoyed a period of prosperity and prominence among European nations. Richelieu established the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acad%C3%A9mie_fran%C3%A7aise" title="Académie française">Académie française</a> to protect the French language. By the early 1800s, Parisian French had become the primary language of the aristocracy in France.
</p><p>Near the beginning of the 19th century, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_government" title="French government">French government</a> began to pursue policies with the end goal of eradicating the many minorities and regional languages (<a href="/wiki/Patois" title="Patois">patois</a>) spoken in France. This began in 1794 with <a href="/wiki/Henri_Gr%C3%A9goire" title="Henri Grégoire">Henri Grégoire</a>'s "Report on the necessity and means to annihilate the patois and to universalize the use of the French language". When public education was made <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_education" title="Compulsory education">compulsory</a>, only French was taught and the use of any other (patois) language was punished. The goals of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_school_system" title="French school system">Public School System</a> were made especially clear to the French-speaking teachers sent to teach students in regions such as <a href="/wiki/Occitania" title="Occitania">Occitania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>. Instructions given by a French official to teachers in the <a href="/wiki/Departments_of_France" title="Departments of France">department</a> of <a href="/wiki/Finist%C3%A8re" title="Finistère">Finistère</a>, in western <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, included the following: "And remember, Gents: you were given your position in order to kill the Breton language".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-43">[43]</a></sup> The prefect of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basses-Pyr%C3%A9n%C3%A9es" title="Basses-Pyrénées">Basses-Pyrénées</a> in the French <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Basque_Country" title="Northern Basque Country">Basque Country</a> wrote in 1846: "Our schools in the Basque Country are particularly meant to replace the <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque language</a> with French..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-43">[43]</a></sup> Students were taught that their ancestral languages were inferior and they should be ashamed of them; this process was known in the Occitan-speaking region as <a href="/wiki/Vergonha" title="Vergonha">Vergonha</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geographical_distribution_of_French_speakers" title="Geographical distribution of French speakers">Geographical distribution of French speakers</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/European_French" title="European French">European French</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Knowledge_of_French_EU_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1242" data-file-width="1212" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Knowledge_of_French_EU_map.svg/220px-Knowledge_of_French_EU_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Knowledge_of_French_EU_map.svg/330px-Knowledge_of_French_EU_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Knowledge_of_French_EU_map.svg/440px-Knowledge_of_French_EU_map.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Knowledge_of_French_EU_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Knowledge of French in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> and candidate countries<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Spoken by 19.71% of the European Union's population, French is the third most widely spoken language in the EU, after English and German and the second most-widely taught language after English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nativeLanguages_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-nativeLanguages-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_France" title="Constitution of France">Constitution of France</a>, French has been the official language of the Republic since 1992,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benet-MartínezHong2014_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benet-MartínezHong2014-46">[46]</a></sup> although the <a href="/wiki/Ordinance_of_Villers-Cotter%C3%AAts" title="Ordinance of Villers-Cotterêts">ordinance of Villers-Cotterêts</a> made it mandatory for legal documents in 1539. France mandates the use of French in official government publications, public education except in specific cases, and legal contracts; advertisements must bear a translation of foreign words.
</p><p>In Belgium, French is an official language at the federal level along with Dutch and German. At the regional level, French is the sole official language of <a href="/wiki/Wallonia" title="Wallonia">Wallonia</a> (excluding a part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Cantons" title="East Cantons">East Cantons</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German-speaking</a>) and one of the two official languages—along with <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>—of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brussels-Capital_Region" title="Brussels-Capital Region">Brussels-Capital Region</a>, where it is spoken by the majority of the population (approx. 80%), often as their primary language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philippevanparijs_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-philippevanparijs-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>French is one of the four official languages of Switzerland, along with German, Italian, and <a href="/wiki/Romansh_language" title="Romansh language">Romansh</a>, and is spoken in the western part of Switzerland, called <a href="/wiki/Romandy" title="Romandy">Romandy</a>, of which Geneva is the largest city. The language divisions in Switzerland do not coincide with political subdivisions, and some <a href="/wiki/Cantons_of_Switzerland" title="Cantons of Switzerland">cantons</a> have bilingual status: for example, cities such as <a href="/wiki/Biel/Bienne" title="Biel/Bienne">Biel/Bienne</a> and cantons such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canton_of_Valais" title="Canton of Valais">Valais</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canton_of_Fribourg" title="Canton of Fribourg">Fribourg</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canton_of_Berne" title="Canton of Berne">Berne</a>. French is the native language of about 23% of the Swiss population, and is spoken by 50%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> of the population.
</p><p>Along with Luxembourgish and German, French is one of the three official languages of <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>, where it is generally the preferred language of business as well as of the different public administrations. It is also the official language of <a href="/wiki/Monaco" title="Monaco">Monaco</a>.
</p><p>At a regional level, French is acknowledged as official language in the <a href="/wiki/Aosta_Valley" title="Aosta Valley">Aosta Valley</a> region of Italy where it is the first language of approximately 30% of the population, while French dialects remain spoken by minorities on the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">Channel Islands</a>. It is also spoken in <a href="/wiki/Andorra" title="Andorra">Andorra</a> and is the main language after <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">Catalan</a> in <a href="/wiki/El_Pas_de_la_Casa" title="El Pas de la Casa">El Pas de la Casa</a>. The language is taught as the primary second language in the German <i>land</i> of <a href="/wiki/Saarland" title="Saarland">Saarland</a>, with French being taught from pre-school and over 43% of citizens being able to speak French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Distribution_of_native_French_speakers_in_6_countries_in_2021.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1132" data-file-width="1866" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Distribution_of_native_French_speakers_in_6_countries_in_2021.png/220px-Distribution_of_native_French_speakers_in_6_countries_in_2021.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Distribution_of_native_French_speakers_in_6_countries_in_2021.png/330px-Distribution_of_native_French_speakers_in_6_countries_in_2021.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Distribution_of_native_French_speakers_in_6_countries_in_2021.png/440px-Distribution_of_native_French_speakers_in_6_countries_in_2021.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Distribution_of_native_French_speakers_in_6_countries_in_2021.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of native French speakers in 6 countries in 2021</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/African_French" title="African French">African French</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Francophone_Africa.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1230" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Francophone_Africa.svg/220px-Francophone_Africa.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Francophone_Africa.svg/330px-Francophone_Africa.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Francophone_Africa.svg/440px-Francophone_Africa.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Francophone_Africa.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#006BA8; color:white;"> </span> Countries usually considered part of Francophone Africa<br/> Their population was 442.1 million in 2020,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2020_data_sheet_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-2020_data_sheet-51">[51]</a></sup> and it is forecast to reach between 845 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and 891 million<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2050_pop_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-2050_pop-53">[53]</a></sup> in 2050.</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#00AAE2; color:black;"> </span> Countries sometimes considered as Francophone Africa</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#008080; color:white;"> </span> Countries that are not Francophone but are Members or Observers of the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_internationale_de_la_Francophonie" title="Organisation internationale de la Francophonie">OIF</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>The majority of the world's French-speaking population lives in Africa. According to a 2018 estimate from the Organisation Internationale de la Francophonie, an estimated 141 million African people spread across 34 countries and territories<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[Note 1]</a></sup> can speak French as either a <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2018_speakers_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-2018_speakers-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2018_methodology_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-2018_methodology-56">[55]</a></sup> This number does not include the people living in non-Francophone African countries who have learned French as a foreign language. Due to the rise of French in Africa, the total French-speaking population worldwide is expected to reach 700 million people in 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> French is the fastest growing language on the continent (in terms of either official or foreign languages).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup>
French is mostly a second language in Africa, but it has become a first language in some urban areas, such as the region of <a href="/wiki/Abidjan" title="Abidjan">Abidjan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> and in <a href="/wiki/Libreville" title="Libreville">Libreville</a>, Gabon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CEFAN_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-CEFAN-61">[60]</a></sup> There is not a single <a href="/wiki/African_French" title="African French">African French</a>, but multiple forms that diverged through contact with various indigenous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_languages" title="African languages">African languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> is the region where the French language is most likely to expand, because of the expansion of education and rapid population growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> It is also where the language has evolved the most in recent years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> Some vernacular forms of French in Africa can be difficult to understand for French speakers from other countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> but written forms of the language are very closely related to those of the rest of the French-speaking world.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_North_America" title="Languages of North America">Languages of North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_South_America" title="Languages of South America">Languages of South America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_Caribbean" title="Languages of the Caribbean">Languages of the Caribbean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/French_language_in_Canada" title="French language in Canada">French language in Canada</a></div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span></h4>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:French_language_distribution_in_Canada.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1263" data-file-width="1304" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/French_language_distribution_in_Canada.png/200px-French_language_distribution_in_Canada.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/French_language_distribution_in_Canada.png/300px-French_language_distribution_in_Canada.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/French_language_distribution_in_Canada.png/400px-French_language_distribution_in_Canada.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">French language distribution in Canada
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#0049a2; color:white;"> </span> Regions where French is the main language</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#006aFF; color:white;"> </span> Regions where French is an official language but not a majority native language</div></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arret.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="456" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Arret.jpg/200px-Arret.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Arret.jpg/300px-Arret.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Arret.jpg/400px-Arret.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Stop_sign" title="Stop sign">"arrêt" signs</a> (French for "stop") are used in Canada while the English <i>stop,</i> which is also a valid French word, is used in France and other French-speaking countries and regions.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>French is the second most common language in Canada, after English, and both are official languages at the federal level. It is the first language of 9.5 million people or 29% and the second language for 2.07 million or 6% of the entire population of Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qu'est-ce_que_la_Francophonie_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Qu'est-ce_que_la_Francophonie-14">[14]</a></sup> French is the sole official language in the province of <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, being the mother tongue for some 7 million people, or almost 80% (2006 Census) of the province<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. About 95% of the people of Quebec speak French as either their first or second language, and for some as their third language. Quebec is also home to the city of <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>, which is the world's fourth-largest French-speaking city, by number of first language speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a> are the only officially bilingual provinces, though full bilingualism is enacted only in New Brunswick, where about one third of the population is Francophone. French is also an official language of all of the territories (<a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yukon" title="Yukon">Yukon</a>). Out of the three, Yukon has the most French speakers, making up just under 4% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> Furthermore, while French is not an official language in <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, the <a href="/wiki/French_Language_Services_Act" title="French Language Services Act">French Language Services Act</a> ensures that provincial services are to be available in the language. The Act applies to areas of the province where there are significant Francophone communities, namely <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Ontario" title="Eastern Ontario">Eastern Ontario</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ontario" title="Northern Ontario">Northern Ontario</a>. Elsewhere, sizable French-speaking minorities are found in southern Manitoba, <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>, Prince Edward Island and the <a href="/wiki/Port_au_Port_Peninsula" title="Port au Port Peninsula">Port au Port Peninsula</a> in Newfoundland and Labrador, where the unique <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_French" title="Newfoundland French">Newfoundland French</a> dialect was historically spoken. Smaller pockets of French speakers exist in all other provinces. The Ontarian city of <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>, the Canadian capital, is also effectively bilingual, as it has a large population of federal government workers, who are required to offer services in both French and English, and is across a river from Quebec, opposite the major city of <a href="/wiki/Gatineau" title="Gatineau">Gatineau</a> with which it forms a single metropolitan area.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:French_in_the_United_States.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1222" data-file-width="1064" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/French_in_the_United_States.png/220px-French_in_the_United_States.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/French_in_the_United_States.png/330px-French_in_the_United_States.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/French_in_the_United_States.png/440px-French_in_the_United_States.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:French_in_the_United_States.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>French language spread in the United States. Counties marked in lighter pink are those where 6–12% of the population speaks French at home; medium pink, 12–18%; darker pink, over 18%. <a href="/wiki/French-based_creole_languages" title="French-based creole languages">French-based creole languages</a> are not included.</div></div></div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a> (2011), French is the fourth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> most spoken language in the United States after English, Spanish, and Chinese, when all forms of French are considered together and all dialects of Chinese are similarly combined. French is the second most spoken language (after English) in the states of <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, it is tied with Spanish for second most spoken if Louisiana French and all creoles such as Haitian are included. French is the third most spoken language (after English and Spanish) in the states of <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-factfinder2.census.gov_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-factfinder2.census.gov-70">[69]</a></sup> Louisiana is home to many distinct French dialects, collectively known as <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_French" title="Louisiana French">Louisiana French</a>. <a href="/wiki/New_England_French" title="New England French">New England French</a>, essentially a variant of <a href="/wiki/Canadian_French" title="Canadian French">Canadian French</a>, is spoken in parts of <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>. <a href="/wiki/Missouri_French" title="Missouri French">Missouri French</a> was historically spoken in <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a> (formerly known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Louisiana" title="Upper Louisiana">Upper Louisiana</a>), but is nearly extinct today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> French also survived in isolated pockets along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_Coast" title="Gulf Coast">Gulf Coast</a> of what was previously French <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_Louisiana" title="Lower Louisiana">Lower Louisiana</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Mon_Louis_Island" title="Mon Louis Island">Mon Louis Island</a>, Alabama and <a href="/wiki/DeLisle,_Mississippi" title="DeLisle, Mississippi">DeLisle, Mississippi</a> (the latter only being discovered by linguists in the 1990s) but these varieties are severely endangered or presumed extinct.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Caribbean">Caribbean</span></h4>
<p>French is one of two official languages in <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> alongside <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Creole" title="Haitian Creole">Haitian Creole</a>. It is the principal language of education, administration, business, and public signage and is spoken by all educated Haitians. It is also used for ceremonial events such as weddings, graduations, and church masses. The vast majority of the population speaks Haitian Creole as their first language; the rest largely speak French as a first language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeGraff2015_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeGraff2015-72">[71]</a></sup> As a <a href="/wiki/French-based_creole_languages" title="French-based creole languages">French Creole language</a>, Haitian Creole draws the large majority of its vocabulary from French, with influences from West African languages, as well as several European languages. It is closely related to Louisiana Creole and the creole from the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_Antilles" title="Lesser Antilles">Lesser Antilles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>French is the sole official language of all the overseas territories of France in the Caribbean that are collectively referred to as the <a href="/wiki/French_West_Indies" title="French West Indies">French West Indies</a>, namely <a href="/wiki/Guadeloupe" title="Guadeloupe">Guadeloupe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Barth%C3%A9lemy" title="Saint Barthélemy">Saint Barthélemy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collectivity_of_Saint_Martin" title="Collectivity of Saint Martin">Saint Martin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Martinique" title="Martinique">Martinique</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_territories">Other territories</span></h4>
<p>French is the official language of both <a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a> on the South American continent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> and of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Pierre_and_Miquelon" title="Saint Pierre and Miquelon">Saint Pierre and Miquelon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> an archipelago off the coast of Newfoundland in North America.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/French_language_in_Vietnam" title="French language in Vietnam">French language in Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language_in_Laos" title="French language in Laos">French language in Laos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/French_language_in_Cambodia" title="French language in Cambodia">French language in Cambodia</a></div>
<p>French was the official language of the colony of <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a>, comprising modern-day <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>. It continues to be an administrative language in Laos and Cambodia, although its influence has waned in recent decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> In colonial Vietnam, the elites primarily spoke French, while many servants who worked in French households spoke a French pidgin known as "<a href="/wiki/T%C3%A2y_B%E1%BB%93i_Pidgin_French" title="Tây Bồi Pidgin French">Tây Bồi</a>" (now extinct). After French rule ended, <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnam</a> continued to use French in administration, education, and trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> But since the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Saigon" title="Fall of Saigon">Fall of Saigon</a> and the opening of a unified Vietnam's economy, French has gradually been effectively displaced as the main foreign language of choice by English in Vietnam. All three countries are full members of La Francophonie (OIF).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_French" title="Indian French">Indian French</a></div>
<p>French was the official language of <a href="/wiki/French_India" title="French India">French India</a>, consisting of geographically separate enclaves now referred to as <a href="/wiki/Puducherry_(union_territory)" title="Puducherry (union territory)">Puducherry</a>. It was the sole <a href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_Puducherry" title="Official languages of Puducherry">official language of Puducherry</a> until its cession to India in 1956 and continued to be so until 1965 because of the Article XXVIII of the 1956 Treaty of Cession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Traité_de_Cession,_1956_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Traité_de_Cession,_1956-78">[77]</a></sup> In 1963, the <i><a href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_Puducherry#1963_Resolution_by_Representative_Assembly" title="Official languages of Puducherry">Pondicherry representative assembly resolution</a></i> voted for continuance of French as official language but with addition of other maternal languages spoken in Puducherry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> In 1965, an act was passed giving official status to Tamil, English, <a href="/wiki/Telugu_language" title="Telugu language">Telugu</a> <i>(in <a href="/wiki/Yanam_district" title="Yanam district">Yanam</a>)</i> and <a href="/wiki/Malayalam" title="Malayalam">Malayalam</a><i> (in <a href="/wiki/Mahe_district" title="Mahe district">Mahe</a>)</i> as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1965act_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-1965act-80">[79]</a></sup> An official mention during 2006 in Parliamentary debates of the <a href="/wiki/Rajya_Sabha" title="Rajya Sabha">Rajya Sabha</a> confirmed that French is still among the official languages of Puducherry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> Nevertheless, today a small number of older locals still retain knowledge of the language although it has now given way to Tamil and English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Asia">Western Asia</span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Lebanon">Lebanon</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/French_language_in_Lebanon" title="French language in Lebanon">French language in Lebanon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bienvenue_a_Rechmaya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="564" data-file-width="843" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bienvenue_a_Rechmaya.jpg/150px-Bienvenue_a_Rechmaya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bienvenue_a_Rechmaya.jpg/225px-Bienvenue_a_Rechmaya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bienvenue_a_Rechmaya.jpg/300px-Bienvenue_a_Rechmaya.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bienvenue_a_Rechmaya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Town sign in <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Standard Arabic</a> and French at the entrance of <a href="/wiki/Rechmaya" title="Rechmaya">Rechmaya</a> in Lebanon</div></div></div>
<p>A former French <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Mandate_for_Syria_and_the_Lebanon" title="French Mandate for Syria and the Lebanon">mandate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> designates <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> as the sole official language, while a special law regulates cases when French can be publicly used. Article 11 of Lebanon's Constitution states that "Arabic is the official national language. A law determines the cases in which the French language is to be used".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-article_11_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-article_11-84">[83]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/French_language_in_Lebanon" title="French language in Lebanon">French language in Lebanon</a> is a widespread second language among the <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_people" title="Lebanese people">Lebanese people</a>, and is taught in many schools along with Arabic and English. French is used on <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_pound" title="Lebanese pound">Lebanese pound</a> banknotes, on road signs, on Lebanese <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_registration_plates_of_Lebanon" title="Vehicle registration plates of Lebanon">license plates</a>, and on official buildings (alongside Arabic).
</p><p>Today, French and English are secondary languages of <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, with about 40% of the population being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francophone" title="Francophone">Francophone</a> and 40% Anglophone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOIF2014217_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOIF2014217-85">[84]</a></sup> The use of English is growing in the business and media environment. Out of about 900,000 students, about 500,000 are enrolled in Francophone schools, public or private, in which the teaching of mathematics and scientific subjects is provided in French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOIF2014218_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOIF2014218-86">[85]</a></sup> Actual usage of French varies depending on the region and social status. One-third of high school students educated in French go on to pursue higher education in English-speaking institutions. English is the language of business and communication, with French being an element of social distinction, chosen for its emotional value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOIF2014358_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOIF2014358-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Arab_Emirates_and_Qatar">United Arab Emirates and Qatar</span></h5>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">UAE</a> has the status in the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_internationale_de_la_Francophonie" title="Organisation internationale de la Francophonie">Organisation internationale de la Francophonie</a> as an observer state, and <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a> has the status in the organization as an associate state. However, in both countries, French is not spoken by almost any of the general population or migrant workers, but spoken by a small minority of those who invest in Francophone countries or have other financial or family ties. Their entrance as observer and associate states respectively into the organization was aided a good deal by their investments into the Organisation and France itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> A country's status as an observer state in the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_internationale_de_la_Francophonie" title="Organisation internationale de la Francophonie">Organisation internationale de la Francophonie</a> gives the country the right to send representatives to organization meetings and make formal requests to the organization but they do not have voting rights within the OIF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> A country's status as an associate state also does not give a country voting abilities but associate states can discuss and review organization matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania_and_Australasia">Oceania and Australasia</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CFP_500_recto.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="254" data-file-width="462" decoding="async" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/CFP_500_recto.jpg/170px-CFP_500_recto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/CFP_500_recto.jpg/255px-CFP_500_recto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/CFP_500_recto.jpg/340px-CFP_500_recto.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CFP_500_recto.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 500-<a href="/wiki/CFP_franc" title="CFP franc">CFP franc</a> (€4.20; US$5.00) banknote, used in <a href="/wiki/French_Polynesia" title="French Polynesia">French Polynesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wallis_and_Futuna" title="Wallis and Futuna">Wallis and Futuna</a></div></div></div>
<p>French is an official language of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Island" title="Pacific Island">Pacific Island</a> nation of <a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">Vanuatu</a>, where 31% of the population was estimated to speak it in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2018_speakers_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-2018_speakers-55">[54]</a></sup> In the French special collectivity of <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>, 97% of the population can speak, read and write French<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> while in <a href="/wiki/French_Polynesia" title="French Polynesia">French Polynesia</a> this figure is 95%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PF_2017_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-PF_2017-92">[91]</a></sup> and in the French collectivity of <a href="/wiki/Wallis_and_Futuna" title="Wallis and Futuna">Wallis and Futuna</a>, it is 84%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WF_2018_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-WF_2018-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In French Polynesia and to a lesser extent Wallis and Futuna, where oral and written knowledge of the French language has become almost universal (95% and 84% respectively), French increasingly tends to displace the native Polynesian languages as the language most spoken at home. In French Polynesia, the percentage of the population who reported that French was the language they use the most at home rose from 67% at the 2007 census to 74% at the 2017 census.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PF_2007_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-PF_2007-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PF_2017_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-PF_2017-92">[91]</a></sup> In Wallis and Futuna, the percentage of the population who reported that French was the language they use the most at home rose from 10% at the 2008 census to 13% at the 2018 census.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WF_2018_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-WF_2018-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WF_2008_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-WF_2008-95">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future">Future</span></h3>
<p>The future of the French language is often discussed in the news. For example, in 2014, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> documented an increase in the teaching of French in New York, especially in K-12 dual-language programs where Spanish and Mandarin are the only second-language options more popular than French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> In a study published in March 2014 by <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i>, the investment bank <a href="/wiki/Natixis" title="Natixis">Natixis</a> said that French could become the world's most spoken language by 2050. It noted that French is spreading in areas where the population is rapidly increasing, especially in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, French was the dominant language within all institutions until the 1990s. After several enlargements of the EU (1995, 2004), French significantly lost ground in favour of English, which is more widely spoken and taught in most EU countries. French currently remains one of the three working languages, or "procedural languages", of the EU, along with English and German. It is the second most widely used language within EU institutions after English, but remains the preferred language of certain institutions or administrations such as the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Justice_of_the_European_Union" title="Court of Justice of the European Union">Court of Justice of the European Union</a>, where it is the sole internal working language, or the <a href="/wiki/Directorate-General_for_Agriculture_and_Rural_Development" title="Directorate-General for Agriculture and Rural Development">Directorate-General for Agriculture</a>. Since 2016, <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">Brexit</a> has rekindled discussions on whether or not French should again hold greater role within the institutions of the European Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_French" title="Varieties of French">Varieties of French</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 22em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/African_French" title="African French">African French</a>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maghreb_French" title="Maghreb French">Maghreb French</a> (North African French)</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aostan_French" title="Aostan French">Aostan French</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Belgian_French" title="Belgian French">Belgian French</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cambodian_French" title="Cambodian French">Cambodian French</a></li>

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Acadian_French" title="Acadian French">Acadian French</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_French" title="Newfoundland French">Newfoundland French</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/New_England_French" title="New England French">New England French</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontario_French" title="Ontario French">Ontario French</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Quebec_French" title="Quebec French">Quebec French</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_in_France" title="French in France">French French</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">Guianese French</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Missouri_French" title="Missouri French">Missouri French</a></li>
